If he looks at the face of the pile, or at the apparent side of the folded cloth, or at the face of the slave-girl, or at the face of the riding animal and its rump, then he has no option. From this it is understood that the seller does not have khiyar al-ru'yah. (3) The athar indicates that the seller does not get khiyar al-ru'yah: From Ibn Abi Mulaykah that 'Uthman bought land from Talhah ibn 'Ubaydullah in Madinah in exchange for land he had in Kufah from the spoils. When they separated, 'Uthman regretted and said, 'I sold something I did not see.' Talhah said, 'The option is for me; I bought an unseen thing. As for you, you saw what you sold.' So they appointed Jubayr ibn Mut'am as arbiter, who ruled against 'Uthman that the sale is valid and the option is for Talhah because he bought an unseen thing. (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, chapter on permissibility of selling absent items, vol. 5, p. 439, no. 10424). In this athar, Jubayr ibn Mut'am did not give the seller 'Uthman khiyar al-ru'yah, but gave it to the buyer Talhah. So it is learned that the seller does not have khiyar al-ru'yah. The principle is: the seller has no option of inspection. (862) (4) If he sees the top of the pile, or the apparent side of the folded cloth, or the face of the slave-girl, or the front and rump of the animal, then he has no option. Commentary: It is not necessary to see every single part of the sale object; rather, seeing that part which is customarily considered as seeing the object suffices, and by seeing that the option of inspection drops. For example, by seeing the top of the pile, knowledge of the whole pile is obtained; similarly, by seeing the face of a slave-girl, knowledge of the whole slave-girl is obtained; by seeing the front and rump of an animal, knowledge of the whole animal is obtained. In short, by seeing that part which gives knowledge of the entire item, the option of inspection drops. 'Matwiy' means folded, 'kafal' means rump. Footnote: (b) From Ibn Abi Mulaykah: 'Uthman bought land from Talhah... [repeat of athar].

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement concerning the khiyar al-ru'yah (option of inspection) when only part of the sale object is seen. The author first presents the ruling: if the buyer sees the customary representative part of an object, he loses his option of inspection. The Urdu commentary elaborates that it is not necessary to inspect every detail; rather, custom ('urf) determines what constitutes 'seeing' the item. For example, seeing the top of a grain pile or the face of a slave-girl gives sufficient knowledge to close the option. The passage then includes an athar (narration) from Ibn Abi Mulaykah involving 'Uthman and Talhah, which is cited as proof that the seller does not have khiyar al-ru'yah; only the buyer of an unseen item has it. The commentary emphasizes this principle: the seller has no option since he already saw the object before selling. The footnote repeats the athar for emphasis.

Key Terms
خیار رویت (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option of the buyer to rescind a contract after seeing the purchased item if it does not match his expectation.
صبرة (sibrah): A pile of grain or similar commodity; seeing its top is sufficient to gain knowledge of the whole.
کفل (kafal): The rump or hindquarters of an animal; seeing the front and rump together gives sufficient knowledge of the animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because Talhah had seen the land before buying.
C) Because Jubayr was biased against 'Uthman.
D) Because the land was in Kufah and not in Madinah.

Key Takeaway
The buyer's option of inspection drops when he sees the part of the object that customarily represents the whole; the seller never has this option.

Ruling on Seeing the Courtyard of a House: Custom and Khiyar al-Ru'yah
States that seeing the courtyard suffices if rooms are uniform in design.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-ru'yah (option of inspection) for a house, distinguishing when seeing the courtyard suffices based on local custom.

Core Idea
In khiyar al-ru'yah, custom determines sufficient inspection; a uniform courtyard gives knowledge of rooms.

This lesson examines a key ruling in the law of sale: the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'yah). The classical text states a simple rule about seeing a house's courtyard, but the commentator expands it by highlighting the role of custom ('urf). Understanding how custom shapes the application of a rule is essential for a sound grasp of Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and its Subject
What is the exact legal statement? The passage says: if the buyer sees only the courtyard, the option of inspection drops, even without seeing the rooms. This is the default ruling for the author's context.
Note that the ruling is conditional on the buyer having a khiyar al-ru'yah (the right to return the item if not seen before purchase).
2
Recognize the Role of Custom ('Urf)
The Urdu commentary explains that this ruling is based on the local custom where rooms were uniform. Seeing the courtyard gave sufficient knowledge of the rooms. Thus, the ruling is not absolute; it is tied to what custom considers adequate inspection.
Hanafi fiqh often incorporates 'urf as a source of law, especially in mu'amalat.
3
Apply the Custom Distinction
If the custom differs (e.g., rooms have unique interiors), the ruling changes: the buyer must see the rooms themselves. This shows that the effective cause (illah) for dropping the option is obtaining knowledge, which is gauged by custom.
This illustrates the Hanafi principle that rulings can vary with time and place based on custom, as long as no primary text is violated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان رأى صحن الدار فلا خيار له وان لم يشاهد بيوتها تشریح مصنف کے ملک میں کمرے ایک طرح کے ہوا کرتے تھے اور محن کو دیکھنے سے کمروں کا اندازہ ہو جاتا تھا اس لئے فرمایا کہ محن کے دیکھنے سے خیار رویت ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ چاہے کمروں کو نہ دیکھا ہو۔ لیکن جن ملکوں میں کمرے کے اندر کا حصہ الگ الگ انداز کا ہو ان ملکوں میں کمرے کے اندر دیکھنا ہوگا۔ اس کے بغیر خیار رویت ساقط نہیں ہو گا وجہ اور اصول او پر گزر گئے۔
If he sees the courtyard of the house, he has no option, even if he does not see its rooms. Commentary: In the author's country, rooms used to be of one type, and seeing the courtyard gave an idea of the rooms, so he said that seeing the courtyard drops the option of inspection, even if he did not see the rooms. But in countries where the interior of rooms is of different types, it will be necessary to see inside the rooms; without that, the option of inspection will not drop. The reason and principle have passed before.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement regarding khiyar al-ru'yah for a house. The author rules that seeing the courtyard alone is sufficient to drop the option, even without inspecting individual rooms. The Urdu commentary explains that this ruling is based on the custom in the author's locality where rooms were uniform; thus, seeing the courtyard gave adequate knowledge of the house. However, in places where rooms vary, merely seeing the courtyard is not enough; the buyer must view the rooms themselves. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that khiyar al-ru'yah is tied to knowledge, and custom ('urf) determines what constitutes sufficient inspection.

Key Terms
صحن الدار (sahn al-dar): The courtyard or open area of a house.
خیار رویت (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option of inspection: a buyer's right to return an item if it does not match what was expected after seeing it.
عرف ('urf): Custom; recurrent practice that is accepted as a source of law in matters not governed by explicit text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) When the rooms are uniform in design so the courtyard gives knowledge
C) Only if the buyer explicitly agrees in the contract
D) When the seller permits it

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the sufficiency of inspecting only the courtyard for khiyar al-ru'yah depends on local custom regarding uniformity of rooms; ruling is not absolute but context-sensitive.

Validity of a Blind Person's Sale and Purchase
States that a blind person's sale and purchase are valid with option

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a blind person's transaction and identify the conditions that trigger khiyar al-ru'yah.

Core Idea
A blind person's sale is valid, but he retains the option of inspection because he could not see the item.

Can a blind person buy or sell? Some might think blindness prevents valid contracts, but the Hanafi school affirms capacity. This lesson examines the ruling and the accompanying right of khiyar al-ru'yah, ensuring the blind person is not disadvantaged by lack of sight.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
What is the legal status of a blind person's sale and purchase? The text states it is valid (ja'iz).
Ja'iz here means sahih (valid) and enforceable.
2
Locate the rationale
Why is the contract valid? Because the blind person is a sane adult with human needs, capable of transacting.
The Urdu commentary emphasizes insani zarurat (human necessity).
3
Recognize the additional right
What extra right does the blind person have? He has khiyar al-ru'yah (option of inspection) when he buys, since he could not see the object.
This khiyar protects against unseen defects.
4
Note the limitations
Can the option be lost? Yes, through other means of perception (touch, smell, etc.) or explicit waiver. Details are given later in the text.
The text hints at ways the option drops.
5
Apply to a case
If a blind man buys a cow described but not seen, what is the ruling? The sale is valid, but he may cancel after seeing the cow if he is not satisfied.
The khiyar vests immediately upon contract.

How a Blind Person's Option of Inspection Drops | يسقط خيار الأعمى بالجس والشم والذوق
Lists alternative perception methods that drop the blind person's option

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the methods by which a blind person's option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'yah) drops in a sale contract, and explain why delegation suffices.

Core Idea
A blind person's option drops by touch, smell, taste, or delegation — sight is not needed to waive the right.

In Hanafi fiqh, khiyar al-ru'yah grants a buyer the right to inspect the purchased item before concluding the sale. For a blind buyer, sight is impossible, so the law provides alternative sensory methods—touch, smell, and taste—to ascertain the item's nature. Additionally, the blind person may appoint a representative (wakil) to inspect on his behalf. This lesson explains these methods and the underlying principle that substitutes suffice when the original act cannot be performed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the right involved: Khiyar al-Ru'yah
The option of inspection is a right for the buyer to see the item before finalizing the sale. For a blind person, this right must be fulfilled by alternative means.
This right is established to prevent deception.
2
List the alternative sensory methods
The text enumerates three methods: touch (jass), smell (shamm), and taste (dhawq). Each method is valid only if the item can be recognized by that sense.
For example, a watermelon can be tapped (touch), perfume smelled, honey tasted.
3
Recognise the delegation method
The blind person may appoint a representative (wakil) to see the item. The representative's inspection is legally attributed to the blind person, dropping the option.
This is analogous to tayammum — when unable to use water, the substitute is valid.
4
Apply the principle of substitution
The commentary draws an analogy to tayammum: when one cannot perform the original act, its substitute is sufficient. Similarly, a blind person cannot use sight, so alternative senses or a representative are valid.
This principle is rooted in the Hanafi maxim: 'Al-umur bi-maqasidiha' (matters are judged by their objectives).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويسقط خياره بان يجس المبيع اذا كان يعرف بالجس او يشمه اذا كان يعرف بالشم او يذوقه اذا كان يعرف بالذوق كما في البصر نابینا کا خیار ساقط ہو جائے گا اس طرح کہ میچ کو ٹولے اگر ٹولنے سے معلوم ہو سکتا ہو، یا اس کو سونگھ لے اگر سونگھنے سے معلوم ہو سکتا ہو، یا چکھ لے اگر چکھنے سے معلوم ہو سکتا ہو جیسا کہ دیکھنے والے آدمی میں ہوتا ہے۔ تشریح نا بینا مشتری دیکھ نہیں سکتا ہے اس لئے مبیع کی حقیقت کو پہچاننے کے لئے جو دوسرے طریقے ہو سکتے ہیں ان کو استعمال کرنے سے اس کا خیار رویت ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ اور اگر سونگھ کر مبیع کی حقیقت معلوم ہو سکتی ہے اور سونگھ کر دیکھ لیا تو خیار ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ اور چکھ کر معلوم کی جاسکتی ہو تو چکھ کر دیکھ لیا تو خیار ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ جس طرح آدمی دکھنے والا ہو اور ٹول کر یا سونگھ کر یا چکھ کر دیکھ لے تو خیار ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ اسی طرح نابینا بھی ایسا کرلے تو خیار ساقط ہو جائے گا ۔ یا پھر میچ دیکھنے کا کسی کو وکیل بنالے اور وکیل دیکھ لے تو نا بینا کا دیکھنا شمار کیا جائے گا اور اس کا خیار ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ اصول اصل پر عمل نہ کر سکتا ہو تو اس کے نائب پر عمل کرنا کافی ہو گا۔ جس طرح وضو پر قدرت نہ ہو تو تیم کرنا کافی ہوگا۔
His option drops by his touching the sold item if it can be recognized by touch, or by smelling it if recognized by smell, or by tasting it if recognized by taste, just as in the case of a sighted person. The blind person's option drops if he touches the item, if it can be known by touch, or smells it, or tastes it, similar to a sighted person. Commentary: A blind buyer cannot see, so by using other means to know the reality of the item, his option of inspection drops. If by smelling the reality can be known and he smells, the option drops. Similarly for taste. Just as a sighted person's option drops by touching, smelling, or tasting, so too the blind person's option drops by those means. Or if he appoints a representative (wakil) to see the item and the wakil sees it, that counts as his seeing and his option drops. The principle: if one cannot perform an act himself, it suffices to do it through a deputy, just as tayammum suffices when unable to use water.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement detailing how a blind person loses his khiyar al-ru'yah. Since he cannot see, he uses other senses: touch, smell, or taste, depending on the item. The Urdu commentary explains that if he touches, smells, or tastes the item in a way that reveals its reality, his option drops. Additionally, he may appoint a representative (wakil) to see the item, and the representative's sight counts as his own. The commentary draws an analogy to tayammum: when one cannot perform the original act, the substitute is sufficient.

Key Terms
جس (jass): To touch or feel an object to identify it.
شم (shamm): To smell an object to identify it.
ذوق (dhawq): To taste an object to identify it.

Key Takeaway
A blind person's khiyar al-ru'yah drops by using alternative senses (touch, smell, taste) or by delegating inspection to a representative.

The Blind Buyer's Option in Real Estate (Al-'Iqar)
Ruling: Blind person's option in real estate remains until described.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule for a blind person's option in real estate and explain why a verbal description is necessary.

Core Idea
A blind buyer's option in land only drops after a detailed verbal description — other senses cannot convey the property.

This lesson examines a specific ruling in Hanafi fiqh concerning the khiyar al-ru'yah (option of inspection) for a blind person when purchasing real estate. Unlike movables that can be known by touch, land requires a thorough description from the seller. We will explore the logic behind this rule and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the purchase of land by a blind person. The ruling is that his option of inspection does not drop until the seller describes the property.
This is a specific exception for real estate.
2
Recognize the general principle
Normally, khiyar al-ru'yah allows the buyer to inspect the item physically. For a sighted person, seeing the property suffices.
3
Understand the exception for blind buyers
A blind person cannot use sight; other senses (touch, smell, taste) are insufficient for land because it is too large and complex.
The commentary explicitly states land cannot be known by those senses.
4
Apply the condition
The seller must give a complete verbal description of the property's attributes. The blind buyer must hear it and express satisfaction.
Only upon hearing and consenting does the option drop.
5
Note the practical effect
If the description is incomplete or the buyer does not clearly consent, the option remains, and the sale is not binding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يسقط خياره في العقار حتى يوصف له نابینا نے زمین خریدی اب اس کو ٹول کر یا سونگھ کر یا چکھ کر معلوم نہیں کر سکتا اس لئے اس کی خیار کے ساقط کرنے اور مکمل رضا مندی کا طریقہ یہ ہے کہ بائع زمین کی پوری حقیقت بیان کرے اور پورا وصف بیان کرے۔ ان کو سن کر نا بینا راضی ہو جائے تو خیار رویت ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ العقار : زمین۔
His option does not drop in real estate until it is described to him. A blind person bought land; now he cannot know it by touching, smelling, or tasting. So the way to drop his option and achieve complete satisfaction is that the seller should describe the full reality and complete attributes of the land. When the blind person hears that and is satisfied, his option of inspection drops. 'Al-'iqar' means land.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This is a ruling statement specific to real estate (al-'iqar) in the context of a blind buyer. Since land cannot be known by touch, smell, or taste, the blind person's khiyar al-ru'yah remains until the seller gives a detailed verbal description of the property. The commentary explains that only upon hearing a complete description and expressing satisfaction does the option drop. This is an exception to the general rule for other items.

Key Terms
العقار (al-'iqar): Real estate, land that cannot be moved.

Key Takeaway
A blind buyer's option in real estate remains until the seller provides a full verbal description and the buyer consents.

The Sale of Another's Property: Suspended, Not Void
States that the owner has the option to ratify or nullify such sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on bay' al-fuduli and identify the condition for the owner's option.

Core Idea
A sale of another's property is suspended on the owner's approval—not void ab initio.

In Hanafi mu'amalat, ownership is a fundamental condition for transferring property. The ruling on selling someone else's property without permission illustrates a key exception: the contract is not automatically void but is suspended, awaiting the owner's decision. This lesson examines that ruling and its condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling property of another without the owner's command. The ruling is that the owner has an option (khiyar) to either ratify (ijazah) or nullify (faskh) the sale.
2
Classify the contract status
Under Hanafi fiqh, such a contract is not batil (void) but mawquf (suspended). It becomes effective only upon the owner's ratification, and if rejected, it becomes void retrospectively.
This differs from the Shafi'i school which considers it invalid ab initio.
3
State the condition for the option
The owner's right to ratify exists only as long as the subject matter (the sold item) remains in its original state and the seller and buyer have not changed their positions (e.g., one of them dies or becomes insane).
This condition ensures the contract is still capable of being ratified.
4
Note the practical application
If the owner ratifies, the sale is valid from the moment of contract. If the owner rejects, the sale is void, and the buyer must return the item to the owner.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و من باع ملک غیره بغیر امره فالمالک بالخیار ان شاء اجاز جس نے غیر کی ملکیت کو اس کے حکم کے بغیر بچا تو مالک کو اختیار ہے چاہے تو بیع کو جائز قرار دے اور چاہے تو ضیح کر دے۔ اور اس کو اجازت دینے کا حق ہے جب تک معقود علیہ یعنی بیع باقی ہو اور بائع اور مشتری اپنی حالت پر ہوں۔
Whoever sells the property of another without his command, the owner has the option: if he wishes, he may ratify it, and if he wishes, he may nullify it. And he has the right to give permission as long as the subject matter (i.e., the sale) remains and the seller and buyer are in their original state.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This is a ruling statement regarding the sale of another's property (bay' al-fuduli). The author rules that such a sale is not absolutely void but is suspended on the owner's consent. The owner has khiyar (option) to either ratify (ijazah) or reject the sale. The commentary clarifies that this right exists as long as the contract object remains unchanged and the parties have not altered their positions. This is consistent with the Hanafi doctrine of 'suspended contract' (al-'aqd al-mawquf).

Key Terms
معقود علیہ (al-ma'qud 'alayh): The subject matter of the contract (the sold item).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Void (batil) immediately
B) Valid and binding
C) Suspended (mawquf) on owner's ratification
D) Invalid only if the owner objects

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school treats an unauthorized sale as suspended pending owner's approval, not void.

Sale by a Blind Person and His Option of Sight
Ruling on validity of sale by a blind person and his khiyar al-rya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a sale by a blind person and identify the conditions that remove his khiyar al-rya.

Core Idea
A blind person's sale is valid; his option of sight is removed by touch, smell, or taste for movables, but only by description for real estate.

This lesson covers a specific ruling in Islamic sales law: the capacity of a blind person to contract. While sight is a key means of inspecting goods, the Hanafi school affirms the validity of the contract and provides alternative methods for the blind buyer to confirm the object, ensuring informed consent and preventing disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states that the sale and purchase by a blind person are valid (jā'iz). Recognize that this is the general hukm.
The ruling is sahih (valid), not fard or wajib.
2
Understand the khiyar
The blind buyer retains the option of sight (khiyar al-rya) after purchase. This option gives him the right to confirm the object's quality.
Khiyar al-rya is a protective right for the buyer who has not seen the item.
3
Analyze khiyar removal for movable goods
For movable goods, the option is removed by using other senses: touching (if he can recognize by touch), smelling (if by smell), or tasting (if by taste). This parallels how a sighted person's option is removed by sight.
The key is that the blind person must be able to recognize the item through that sense.
4
Analyze khiyar removal for real estate
For real estate (al-'aqar), touching, smelling, or tasting are insufficient. The option is only removed when the seller describes the property fully to the blind buyer.
Real estate cannot be fully perceived by the other senses, so description is required for informed consent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيع الاعمى وشراؤه جائز وله الخيار اذا اشترى ويسقط خياره بان يجس المبيع اذا كان يعرف بالجس او يشمه اذا كان يعرف بالشم او يذوقه اذا كان يعرف بالذوق كما في البصر ولا يسقط خياره في العقار حتى يوصف له
The sale and purchase of a blind person are valid, and he has the option [of sight] when he purchases. And his option is removed by his touching the sold item if he can recognize by touch, or smelling it if he can recognize by smell, or tasting it if he can recognize by taste, just as with the sighted person. And his option is not removed in real estate until it is described to him.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi ruling on a blind person entering into a sale contract. The sale is valid because he is a sane adult with legal capacity. However, since he cannot see the object, he retains the option of sight (khiyar al-rya) after the contract. This option is removed not by sight, but by his employing other senses that can identify the object: touching if he recognizes by touch, smelling if by smell, or tasting if by taste, exactly as a sighted person's option is removed by sight. For real estate (al-'aqar), which cannot be fully perceived by these senses, the option is not removed until the seller describes it completely to him, ensuring informed consent.

Key Terms
الاعمى (al-a'ma): A person who is blind, lacking sight.
الخيار (al-khiyar): Option or choice; specifically here, khiyar al-rya (option of inspection) granted to the buyer who has not seen the object.
يجس (yajuss): To touch or feel an object to determine its quality.
يشم (yashum): To smell an object to recognize it.
يذوق (yadhuq): To taste an object to recognize it.
العقار (al-'iqqar): Real estate, immovable property such as land or buildings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How is a blind person's option of sight (khiyar al-rya) removed for real estate according to this passage?
A) By touching the property
B) By smelling the property
C) By the seller describing the property fully
D) By the buyer sighting the property through a representative

Key Takeaway
A blind person's sale is valid, and his option of sight is removed by the same senses he uses to identify the object, except for real estate which requires a full description.

Sale of Another's Property Without Permission: Bay' al-Fuduli
Ruling on sale of another's property without permission with proofs and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on bay' al-fuduli, explain the Hanafi proof from the hadith of 'Urwa, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the hadith 'do not sell what is not with you'.

Core Idea
A sale made without the owner's permission is suspended, not void—it becomes binding upon the owner's ratification.

This passage addresses a key point of divergence between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: whether a person can sell something they do not own. The Hanafi school, relying on a practical Sunnah, considers such a sale valid but suspended on the owner's approval. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the proof, and the opposing view, helping you understand the legal reasoning behind each position.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the sale of another's property without permission (bay' al-fuduli) is valid but suspended (mawquf) on the owner's ratification. The owner has the choice to validate or annul.
2
Identify the Hanafi proof
Locate the hadith of 'Urwa: the Prophet (ﷺ) gave him a dinar to buy a sheep; he bought two, sold one, and returned with a dinar and a sheep. The Prophet prayed for blessing in his sale, implicitly approving the unauthorized sale.
This shows the Prophet's tacit approval of an act of fuduli.
3
State the Shafi'i position and proof
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that bay' al-fuduli is invalid and cannot be ratified. His proof is the hadith: 'Do not sell what is not with you' (لا تبع ما ليس عندك).
This hadith is general and appears to forbid selling what one does not own.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school interprets 'what is not with you' as what is not physically present or guaranteed, not what is not owned. The fuduli seller has the capacity to deliver and the sale is subject to ratification, so it is not a sale of what is not 'with' him in the sense of being able to deliver.
The dispute turns on the interpretation of the hadith.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: In Hanafi law, a person can purchase goods without the owner's permission and later seek ratification; in Shafi'i law, such a transaction is void from the outset and cannot be later validated.
This affects everyday transactions, e.g., a broker selling goods without explicit authorization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و من باع ملک غیره بغیر امره فالمالک بالخیار ان شاء اجاز البيع وان شاء فسخ وله الاجازة اذا كان المعقود عليه باقيا والمتعاقدان بحالهما عن عروة ان النبي له اعطاه دينارا يشترى له به شاة فاشترى له به شاتین فباع احدهما بدينار فجائه بدينار وشاة فدعا له بالبركة في بیعه ذكر عبد الله بن عمر قال قال رسول الله الله لا يحل سلف و بيع ولا شرطان في بيع ولا ربح مالم يضمن ولا بيع ما ليس عندک
And whoever sells the property of another without his permission, then the owner has the choice: if he wishes he may validate the sale, and if he wishes he may annul it. And he has the right to validate as long as the object of contract remains and the two contracting parties are in their [same] state. From 'Urwa: that the Prophet (ﷺ) gave him a dinar to buy a sheep for him, so he bought two sheep with it, then sold one of them for a dinar, and came to him with a dinar and a sheep, and he (ﷺ) prayed for blessing in his sale. 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar mentioned that the Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) said: 'A loan combined with a sale is not lawful, nor two conditions in one sale, nor profit on what is not guaranteed, nor the sale of what is not with you.'
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage covers the ruling of bay' al-fuduli (sale by an unauthorized person). The Hanafi position is that such a sale is suspended (mawquf) on the owner's permission. The owner may either ratify the sale or annul it. This is permissible as long as the goods still exist and the parties remain in their same legal capacity. The proof is the hadith of 'Urwa: the Prophet (ﷺ) gave him a dinar to buy a sheep; 'Urwa bought two sheep with it and sold one, returning with a dinar and a sheep, and the Prophet (ﷺ) prayed for blessing, implicitly approving the unauthorized sale. The opposing view (Imam al-Shafi'i) argues from the hadith 'do not sell what is not with you', meaning one cannot sell what he does not own. The Hanafi response is that 'what is not with you' means what is not physically present or guaranteed, not what one does not own. The Hanafi school accepts bay' al-fuduli as valid after ratification.

Key Terms
بيع فضولي (bay' al-fuduli): Sale of another's property without his prior permission; a suspended contract requiring ratification.
المعقود عليه (al-ma'qud 'alayhi): The object of the contract, i.e., the goods sold.
المتعاقدان (al-muta'aqidan): The two contracting parties: the seller and the buyer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens when someone sells another's property without permission?
A) The sale is void and cannot be ratified
B) The sale is suspended and depends on the owner's approval
C) The buyer automatically becomes owner
D) The seller is punished without any option for the owner

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an unauthorized sale (bay' al-fuduli) is a valid suspended contract that becomes binding upon the owner's ratification, supported by the Prophet's approval of 'Urwa's transaction.

Option of Sight for Bundled Purchase
Ruling on khiyar al-rya when one of two items is seen before buying both

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for khiyar al-rya when two items are bought together after seeing only one, and identify the condition that triggers the unified option.

Core Idea
A single contract covering multiple items creates a unified option of sight for all items if part is unseen.

Khiyar al-rya (option of sight) allows a buyer to reject goods not seen at the time of contract. But what if the buyer purchases multiple items in one sale, having seen only one of them? This lesson clarifies that the lack of sight for part of the bundle gives the buyer a unified option over the entire purchase.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the right
The buyer sees one of two garments and purchases both in a single sale. Under khiyar al-rya, the buyer has the right to reject goods not seen before the contract.
2
Determine the scope of the option
Because the sale is a single contract covering both items, the option applies to the entire bundle. The buyer cannot separate the seen from the unseen.
The unity of the contract is key.
3
Apply the ruling
Upon seeing the second garment, the buyer may either keep both or return both. He cannot keep one and return the other.

Source Text

And whoever sees one of two garments and purchases them both, then sees the other, it is permissible for him to return both.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage states a rule regarding khiyar al-rya (option of sight) when purchasing multiple items, only one of which is seen before the contract. If a person sees one of two garments and then buys both together in a single sale, he has the right after seeing the unseen garment to return both garments. This is because the sale is considered one contract, and the lack of sight for part of the object gives the buyer a unified option. He cannot keep the seen one and reject the unseen; the option applies to the entire bundle.

Key Terms
ثوب (thawb): A garment, cloth, or piece of clothing.
يردهما (yarudduhuma): He returns them both (the two garments) to the seller.

Key Takeaway

The Ruling on Bay' al-Fuduli (Unauthorized Sale)
Ruling on unauthorized sale with proof and Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on bay' al-fuduli, explain the Shafi'i objection, and evaluate the Hanafi response.

Core Idea
A sale without the owner's permission is valid but suspended – ratification completes the contract.

In this lesson, we examine the controversial case of the unauthorized sale (bay' al-fuduli). The Hanafi school upholds its validity, while Imam al-Shafi'i rejects it. We will explore the textual proofs from authentic hadith and the rational arguments that support each position. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to weigh evidence in contract law.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling on bay' al-fuduli according to the Hanafi school: valid but suspended on ratification.
The official term is 'sahih mawquf'.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i holds the sale invalid (batil) because the seller does not own the object at the time of contract.
This is a well-known inter-madhhab ikhtilaf.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i dalil: the hadith 'لا يحل بيع ما ليس عندك' (Do not sell what you do not possess).
This hadith is in Abu Dawud and Tirmidhi.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal: the prohibition applies to the time of contract; the sale becomes effective at ratification when ownership is established.
The existence of the object at ratification is a condition, not at contract time.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical consequence: under Hanafi, the owner may ratify and the sale is binding; under Shafi'i, the sale is void ab initio.
This difference affects everyday transactions where agents act without explicit prior authorization.

انوں فضولی کی بیع جائز ہے۔
امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ فضولی کی بیع جائز نہیں ہے۔
کیونکہ اس کے پاس طبیع نہیں ہے۔ اور حدیث میں ہے کہ جس کے پاس صبیح نہ ہو اس کے لئے بیچنا جائز نہیں ہے۔ حدیث میں ہے ذکر عبد الله بن عمر قال قال رسول الله الله لا يحل سلف و بيع ولا شرطان في بيع ولا ربح مالم يضمن ولا بيع ما ليس عندک (ب) ( ابو داؤد شریف، باب فی الرجل پیچ مالیس عنده ص ۱۳۹ نمبر ۳۵۰۴ / ترمذی شریف نمبر ۱۲۳۲) اس حدیث سے پتہ چلا کہ جو مبیع آدمی کے پاس نہ ہو اس کا بیچنا جائز نہیں ۔ اس لئے فضولی کی بیع جائز نہیں ہے۔
طبیع موجود ہو تب اجازت دے سکتا ہے اس کی قید اس لئے ہے کہ مالک کی اجازت کے بعد بیچ ہوگی۔ پس اگر مبیع موجود نہ ہو تو بیچ کس پر ہوگی؟ اس لئے اجازت کے لئے طبیع کا موجود ہونا ضروری ہے۔ اسی طرح اگر بائع موجود ہو گا اور مشتری موجود ہو گا اور وہ اپنی حالت پر ہوں یعنی اجازت کے وقت عاقل، بالغ اور مالک ہوں تب بیچ ہوگی ورنہ نہیں ہوگی۔ اس لئے متعاقدین یعنی بائع اور مشتری کو اپنی حالت پر ہونا ضروری ہے۔ مثلا وہ عاقل ہی نہ رہے، مجنون ہو جائے تو اب اس کی جانب سے بیچ کیسے ہوگی۔

حاشیہ : (الف) آپ نے حضرت عروہ کو ایک دینار دیا تا کہ اس سے ایک بکری خریدے۔ انہوں نے اس ایک دینار سے دو بکریاں خرید لی، پھر ایک بکری کو ایک دینار میں بیچا۔ پس حضور کے پاس ایک دینار اور ایک بکری لے کر آئے۔ آپ نے ان کے لئے بیچ میں برکت کی دعا کی (ب) آپ نے فرمایا ادھار بیچنا اور ساتھ ہی بیچ کرنا حلال نہیں۔ بینچ میں مخالف قسم کی دو شرطیں لگا نا میچ نہیں ، جب تک ضامن نہ ہو اس سے نفع اٹھانا صحیح نہیں۔ اور جو کچھ تمہارے پاس نہ ہو اس کا بیچا صحیح نہیں۔
The sale: if he wishes, he may cancel, and he has the option to ratify when the sold object remains and the two contracting parties are in their original state. Explanation: If someone sells another's property without his permission, his sale is valid because he is sane and adult, and the object is owned and sold, and he sold it not for himself but for the owner. Now the owner may either ratify the sale or cancel it if he does not like it. This sale is called bay' al-fuduli (unauthorized sale). The proof for this sale is in the hadith: from 'Urwa that the Prophet gave him a dinar to buy a sheep, so he bought two sheep with it, then sold one for a dinar, and came back with a dinar and a sheep. The Prophet prayed for blessing in his trade. In this hadith, 'Urwa bought two sheep with one dinar without the Prophet's command, then sold one sheep without command and returned with one dinar profit. The Prophet prayed for him, meaning he approved and validated the sale. This shows that bay' al-fuduli is valid. It also shows that the owner has the choice to keep or cancel the sale later. So bay' al-fuduli is valid. Imam al-Shafi'i says that bay' al-fuduli is not valid because the seller does not possess the object (mabi'). And there is a hadith: 'It is not lawful to sell what you do not possess.' This hadith indicates that selling what one does not possess is not valid, so bay' al-fuduli is not valid. The condition that the object must exist for ratification is because after the owner's permission, the sale will be valid. So if the object does not exist, on what basis would the sale be? Therefore, the existence of the object is necessary for ratification. Similarly, if the seller exists and the buyer exists and they are in their original state (i.e., sane, adult, and owners) at the time of ratification, then the sale will be valid; otherwise not. So the two contracting parties (seller and buyer) must be in their original state. For example, if they become insane, how could the sale be from their side?
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute on the validity of bay' al-fuduli, the sale of another's property without prior authorization. The Hanafi position is that such a sale is valid but suspended on the owner's ratification. The proof is the hadith of 'Urwa, where the Prophet ratified an unauthorized sale by 'Urwa, demonstrating permissibility. The Shafi'i objection cites the hadith prohibiting sale of what one does not possess. The Hanafi response distinguishes: the seller in fuduli does possess the object at the time of ratification (when the owner ratifies, the sale becomes effective). The existence of the object at ratification is a condition, not at the time of contract. Thus, the fuduli sale is valid subject to ratification, and the owner's choice to accept or cancel is established.

Key Terms
بیع فضولی (bay' al-fuduli): A sale of another's property without the owner's prior permission, which is valid but suspended on ratification.
معقود علیہ (ma'qud alayh): The object of the contract; what is being sold.
متعاقدان (muta'aqidan): The two contracting parties: seller and buyer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) No, it is invalid because the Prophet said do not sell what you do not possess.
B) Yes, it is valid but suspended on the owner's ratification.
C) Yes, it is immediately binding without need for ratification.
D) It is invalid unless the seller later purchases the object.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates the unauthorized sale subject to the owner's ratification, distinguishing between contract and ratification conditions.

Khiyar al-Ru'ya When One of Two Items Seen
Ruling on khiyar al-ru'ya when one of two items seen

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a buyer sees only one of two items in a single sale and apply the principle of indivisibility of the contract to the option of sight.

Core Idea
When a sale includes multiple items, an option affecting part extends to the whole.

This passage addresses a common scenario: a buyer sees one garment, then purchases two together. Upon seeing the second, he is dissatisfied. The ruling clarifies that he can return both or keep both, not just the unseen one. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that a single sale contract cannot be partially rescinded when an option applies to part of the sold items.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The buyer saw one of two garments, then bought both in a single sale. When he later saw the other garment, he was dissatisfied. The ruling: he may return both garments or keep both.
The act is a sale of two items with khiyar al-ru'ya for one.
2
Understand the reason for the option
The two garments are distinct; seeing one does not suffice for the other. Therefore, the buyer has khiyar al-ru'ya for the unseen garment upon seeing it.
Khiyar al-ru'ya is established for the unseen item.
3
Recognize the unity of the contract
Since it is a single sale, returning only one garment would leave half the sold item with the buyer and half returned. This is impermissible, so the buyer must either return both or keep both.
The contract is indivisible regarding rescission.
4
Support from athar
An athar from al-Sha'bi states that if a defect is found in some of a group purchase, the buyer must return all or take all. The same principle applies here by analogy.
This reinforces the principle of indivisibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ ۸۶۸] (۱۰) ومن رأى احد الثوبين فاشتراهما ثم رأى الآخر جاز له ان يردهما.
کسی نے دو کپڑوں میں سے ایک کو دیکھا پھر دونوں کو خرید لیا پھر دوسرے کپڑے کو دیکھا تو اس کے لئے جائز ہے کہ دونوں کپڑوں کو واپس کر دے۔
صرف ایک کپڑے کو دیکھا تھا اور ایک ہی بیع میں دوسرے کپڑے کو بھی خرید لیا جس کو دیکھا نہیں تھا تو دوسرے کپڑے کے خیار رویت کے ماتحت دونوں کپڑوں کو واپس کر سکتا ہے۔
دونوں کپڑے مختلف ہیں۔ ایک کو دیکھنا دوسرے کے لئے کافی نہیں ہے اس لئے دوسرے کپڑے میں خیار رویت ملے گا اور چونکہ بیچ ایک ہی ہے اور ایک کپڑے کو واپس کرے گا تو آدھی مبیع رہ جائے گی اور آدھی واپس ہوگی اس لئے واپس کرے گا تو دونوں اور رکھے گا تو دونوں کپڑے۔ اثر میں ہے عن الشعبي في رجل اشترى رقيقا جملة فوجد بعضهم عيبا قال يردهم جميعا او يأخذهم جميعا مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب الرجل یشتری اسمیع جملة فيجد في بعضہ عیاج ٹامن ص ۱۵۶ نمبر ۱۴۶۹۹) اس اثر میں ہے کہ تمام مبیع لے یا تمام چھوڑ
یہاں یہ اصول جاری ہے کہ مبیع مختلف ہیں اس لئے ایک کو دیکھنا دوسرے کے لئے کافی نہیں۔ اس لئے دوسرے میں خیار رویت ملے گا (۲) پوری مبیع واپس ہوگی آدھی نہیں۔
And whoever saw one of the two garments, then bought both, then saw the other, it is permissible for him to return both. Explanation: If someone saw one of two garments, then bought both in a single sale, then saw the other, he may return both garments. Since he only saw one garment and bought the other without seeing it, he has khiyar al-ru'ya for the unseen garment, and under that option he may return both. Because the two garments are distinct; seeing one does not suffice for the other. Therefore, he has the option for the second garment. Since the sale is one, if he returns one, half the sold item would remain and half returned. Hence, he either returns both or keeps both. There is an athar from al-Sha'bi regarding a man who bought a group of slaves and found a defect in some, he said: 'Return all or take all.' (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq). The principle here is that the sold items are distinct, so seeing one does not suffice for the other; thus, the option applies to the unseen, and the entire sale must be either fully returned or fully kept.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This lesson presents a ruling on khiyar al-ru'ya (option of sight) when two items are bought together but only one has been seen. The buyer has the right to return both because he had an option for the unseen item, and since the contract is indivisible, he cannot return only part. This is based on the principle that when items are sold in a single contract, the buyer cannot choose to keep some and return others if there is an option affecting part. The athar from al-Sha'bi supports this: if a defect is found in some of a group purchase, the buyer must return all or keep all. Here, the option of sight is similar: the unseen garment gives the buyer the right to rescind, but due to the unity of the contract, it applies to the entire sale.

Key Terms
خیار رویت (khiyar al-ru'ya): The option for the buyer to cancel a sale upon seeing the purchased item if it does not meet expectations.
مبیع (mabi'): The sold item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He can only return the second, unseen garment.
B) He can return both garments.
C) He must keep both since he agreed to the sale.
D) He can return only the first, seen garment.

Key Takeaway
A sale of multiple items in one contract is indivisible; khiyar al-ru'ya for part applies to the whole.

Death Invalidates Khiyar al-Ru'ya
Ruling that khiyar al-ru'ya does not pass to heirs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that khiyar al-ru'ya becomes void upon the death of the buyer and explain why it is not inherited by the heirs.

Core Idea
Khiyar al-ru'ya is a personal right; it dies with the holder and does not transfer to heirs.

In Hanafi fiqh, the option of sight (khiyar al-ru'ya) allows a buyer who purchases an item unseen to cancel the sale upon seeing it. However, this right is tied to the buyer's own knowledge and decision. If the buyer dies before exercising the option, the right ceases to exist. This lesson explains why it is not inherited and how it differs from financial rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
A person purchases an item without seeing it, having the right to cancel after inspection. The buyer dies before exercising that right.
The seller is still alive and the item remains unsold in the sense of cancellation possible.
2
State the ruling
The option (khiyar al-ru'ya) becomes void (bāṭil). The sale becomes binding on the heirs; they cannot cancel.
The option is not transferred to the heirs.
3
Understand the reasoning (ta‘līl)
Khiyar al-ru'ya is a non-material (ma‘nawī) right—a matter of choice and intention. Such rights depend on the individual and do not survive death.
Contrast with financial rights like khiyar al-‘ayb (option of defect), which can be inherited.
4
Apply the principle to similar cases
The same rule applies to any personal option that is not a financial right, e.g., khiyar al-shart (option of condition) if it is also personal? In Hanafi fiqh, khiyar al-shart can be inherited under certain conditions, but khiyar al-ru'ya is distinct.
This reinforces the need to identify the nature of the right.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۸۶۹] (١١) ومن مات وله خيار الرؤية بطل خياره
خیار رویت ایک معنوی چیز ہے اور اختیار اور ارادے کا نام ہے۔ اور معنوی چیز دوسرے کی طرف منتقل نہیں ہوتی ہے۔ اس لئے مرنے کے بعد یہ اختیار ورثہ کی طرف منتقل نہیں ہوگا۔ باطل ہو جائے گا۔
And whoever dies and he has the option of sight, his option becomes void. Explanation: Khiyar al-ru'ya is a non-material thing; it is a matter of choice and intention. A non-material thing does not transfer to another. Therefore, after death, this option does not transfer to the heirs; it becomes void.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage states that khiyar al-ru'ya is a personal right that cannot be inherited. The reasoning is that the option is a legal capacity that is tied to the individual's own knowledge and decision. Since it is a non-material matter, it does not pass to heirs upon death. Thus, if a buyer dies before exercising the option, his heirs cannot claim it; the sale becomes binding. This is in contrast to rights like the option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb), which can be inherited because it is a financial right.

Key Terms
خیار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'ya): The option of sight; the right to cancel a sale upon seeing the purchased item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to khiyar al-ru'ya if the buyer dies before exercising it?
A) It transfers to the heirs.
B) It becomes void.
C) It gives the seller the option to cancel.
D) The sale is automatically canceled.

Key Takeaway
Khiyar al-ru'ya is a personal non-material right that terminates with the death of the holder; it is not inherited.

Effect of Re-seeing Item on Khiyar al-Ru'ya
Ruling on khiyar al-ru'ya based on prior sight and condition change

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that a prior sight drops khiyar al-ru'ya only if the item's condition is unchanged.

Core Idea
A prior sight that reveals the item's true condition eliminates the option; a changed condition revives it.

This lesson clarifies when a buyer's earlier viewing of an item removes his right to cancel the sale (khiyar al-ru'ya) upon purchase. The central point is that the prior sight must have given him true knowledge of the item's reality at the time of contracting. If the item remains the same, the option is lost; if it has changed, the buyer retains the option. We will examine the ruling, its rationale, and the supporting evidence from the athar of Ibn Sirin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Condition
The author states: if a buyer sees an item, then sees it again after a period (or buys it sight-unseen), the ruling depends on whether the item is unchanged. If unchanged, no option; if changed, option remains.
Focus on the condition: the item must be on the same description (condition) as the prior sight.
2
Understand the Rationale: Knowledge of Reality
The reason is that a sight that gives true knowledge of the sold item's condition satisfies the purpose of khiyar al-ru'ya (to avoid deception). If the item is unchanged, the prior sight already gave that knowledge, so the option is moot.
The Hanafi principle: khiyar al-ru'ya is meant to protect the buyer from buying something he did not truly know.
3
Apply the Condition: Unchanged vs. Changed
Consider a scenario: a buyer saw a cloth six months ago, now buys it. If the cloth is exactly as it was, the earlier sight suffices, no option. But if the cloth has faded or torn, the earlier sight does not reflect the current state, so the option stands.
The time gap is irrelevant; only whether the condition changed matters.
4
Examine the Proof: Athar of Ibn Sirin
The passage cites the statement of Ibn Sirin: 'If a man buys something from you upon a description and you do not contradict what you described, then the sale is binding upon him.' This shows that matching description (or prior sight) makes the sale binding, implying no option.
The athar is used as indirect evidence (qawl sahabi) that a sight that informs the buyer of the reality drops the option.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ ۸۷۰] (۱۲) ومن رأى شيئا ثم رآه بعد مدة فان هو على صفته التي رآه بها فلا خيار له وان وجده قد تغير فالمشتری بالخيار
تش اس رویت سے خیار رویت ساقط ہوگا جس سے مبیع کی حقیقت کا علم ہو جائے۔ اور اگر مبیع کی حقیقت کا علم نہ ہوتو وہ رویت اختیار کے ساقط کرنے کے لئے کافی نہیں۔ اب اگر مثلا چھ ماہ پہلے ایک چیز کو دیکھا تھا اور اسی حال پر وہ مبیع موجود ہے تو پہلی رویت حقیقت معلوم کرنے کے لئے کافی ہے۔ اس لئے مشتری کو خیار رویت نہیں ملے گا۔ اس اثر میں اس کا ثبوت ہے عن ابن سيرين قال اذا ابتاع رجل منک شيئا على صفة فلم تخالف ما وصفت له فقد وجب عليه البيع (الف) (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب البيع على الصفہ وہی نامجبتہ ج ٹامن ص ۴۴ نمبر ۱۴۲۳۷) اس اثر میں ہے کہ اسی صفت پر موجود ہے تو خیار رویت نہیں ملے گا ۔ بیع واجب ہوگی۔ اور اگر مبیع کی حالت بدل گئی ہے تو پہلی رویت حقیقت معلوم کرنے کے لئے کافی نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے مشتری کو خیار رویت ملے گا۔ اور اس کے ماتحت مبیع کو واپس کرنے کا حق ہوگا۔
حاشیہ : (الف) حضرت ابن سیرین سے مروی ہے کہ کوئی آدمی آپ سے کوئی چیز خریدے کسی صفت پر اور جو صفت بیان کی اس کے خلاف نہیں کیا تو اس پر بیچ واجب ہو گئی۔
And whoever saw something then saw it again after a period: if it is upon the description he saw it with, then there is no option for him; and if he finds it changed, then the buyer has the option. Explanation: The option of sight falls by a sight that gives knowledge of the reality of the sold item. If the sight does not give knowledge of its reality, it is not sufficient to drop the option. For example, if he saw something six months ago and it remains in that state, the first sight is sufficient to know its reality, so the buyer has no option. The proof is the athar of Ibn Sirin: 'If a man buys something from you upon a description and you do not contradict what you described, then the sale is binding upon him.' So if the item is as described, no option; if it has changed, the previous sight is insufficient, so the buyer has the option to return.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This lesson explains when a prior sight of an item removes the khiyar al-ru'ya. The ruling is that if the buyer had seen the item earlier and it remains in the same condition, the earlier sight is sufficient to inform him of the item's reality, so he cannot claim the option upon purchase. However, if the item has changed, the earlier sight is not indicative of the current state, so the option remains. The proof is the athar of Ibn Sirin, which ties the binding nature of the sale to the item matching the description. This distinguishes between a sight that gives knowledge (thus dropping the option) and one that does not (preserving the option).

Key Terms
خیار رویت (khiyar al-ru'ya): The option of sight; the right to cancel a sale upon seeing the item if it does not match expectations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because he bought without seeing it at the time of purchase.
B) No, because his previous sight gave him knowledge of its condition.
C) Yes, because the option is always available.
D) Only if the price changed.

Key Takeaway
A prior sight that truly acquainted the buyer with the item's condition drops khiyar al-ru'ya; a sight that does not (due to change) preserves it.

Khiyar al-Ru'ya: When Sight Drops the Option
Ruling on khiyar al-ru'ya: sight that reveals reality drops option

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which khiyar al-ru'ya is dropped and apply it to a sale scenario.

Core Idea
Only sight that gives complete knowledge of the item drops the option of sight.

The option of sight (khiyar al-ru'ya) protects a buyer who purchases unseen goods. Once the buyer sees the item and gains full knowledge of its condition, the right to rescind ends. But if the sight is incomplete or the item differs, the option remains. This lesson explains the precise condition for dropping the option.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act
Khiyar al-ru'ya is a right for the buyer to rescind a sale after seeing the item, if it does not match the pre-sale description. Note that it applies only when the buyer purchased without prior sight.
The option is not for defects but for unseen condition.
2
State the ruling from the passage
The passage says: if the buyer sees the item after purchase and it is as he saw it (i.e., matches the description), he has no option. If he finds it changed, he has the option.
3
Specify the condition for dropping the option
The Urdu commentary clarifies that the sight must provide knowledge of the item's reality (haqiqah). Only such sight is sufficient to drop the option.
A superficial or incomplete sight does not give full knowledge.
4
Distinguish sufficient vs. insufficient sight
If the buyer later sees the item and gains complete knowledge of its characteristics, the option is dropped. If the sight is partial, unclear, or the item has changed, the option remains.
The key is 'knowledge of reality'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اشتراه بعد مدة فان كان على الصفة التى راهِ فلا خيار له وان وجده متغيرا فله الخيار.
ں حقیقت کو معلوم کرنے والی رویت خیار رویت ساقط کرنے کے لئے کافی ہوتی ہے (۲) جو رویت حقیقت کا علم نہ دے وہ خیار رویت ساقط کرنے کے لئے کافی نہیں۔
He bought it after a period, and if it is as he saw it, he has no option; if he finds it changed, he has the option. (Urdu:) The sight that provides knowledge of the reality is sufficient to drop the option of sight. (2) The sight that does not provide knowledge of the reality is not sufficient to drop the option of sight.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on khiyar al-ru'ya (option of sight). When a buyer purchases an item without seeing it initially, then later sees it, if the item matches the description seen earlier, the buyer loses the option to rescind the sale. If the item has changed from what was seen, the buyer retains the option. The Urdu commentary clarifies that only sight which gives true knowledge of the item's condition can nullify the option; a superficial or incomplete sight does not suffice. This ruling is based on the principle that khiyar al-ru'ya protects the buyer from unseen defects, but once the buyer's knowledge is complete, the option ceases.

Key Terms
خیار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'ya): The option for the buyer to rescind a sale after seeing the item, if it does not match the pre-sale description.
رویت (ru'ya): Sight or visual inspection that provides knowledge of the item's actual condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When he sees the item regardless of condition
B) When he sees the item and it is exactly as described
C) When the seller claims it is unchanged
D) When the buyer delays inspection

Key Takeaway
Khiyar al-ru'ya is dropped only when the buyer's sight gives complete knowledge of the item as it was described; otherwise, the option remains.

Khiyar al-‘Ayb: The Option for Defect
Ruling on khiyar al-ayb with hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the buyer's options when a defect is discovered in a purchased item and apply the Hanafi ruling that only return or acceptance with full price is allowed.

Core Idea
A buyer cannot keep a defective item and claim compensation — only return or acceptance with full price.

When you buy something and later find a hidden defect, you may feel entitled to a discount. But what does the Shari'a say? This lesson presents the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-ayb, supported by a hadith of the Prophet ﷺ that establishes the principle 'profit is with liability.' We will walk through the ruling, its proof, and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the buyer's options
From the text, determine what a buyer may do upon discovering a defect. Note both permissible actions and what is not allowed.
The text explicitly states two options: keep with full price or return. It prohibits keeping and taking compensation.
2
Analyze the hadith proof
Read the hadith from Aisha. Observe that the Prophet ordered the return of a defective slave and responded to the seller's complaint about profit with the maxim 'al-kharaaj bi al-daman.' Understand how this supports the ruling.
The hadith is a dalil naqli from a hadith ahad.
3
Understand the maxim 'al-kharaaj bi al-daman'
This maxim means profit accompanies liability. Since the buyer bore the risk of loss while the item was in his possession, any profit generated belongs to him. This explains why he can return the item without compensating for profit.
The maxim is also applicable in other areas of fiqh.
4
Note the conditions for khiyar al-ayb
From the commentary, identify conditions: the defect must be considered significant by merchants, the buyer must not have seen it or agreed to it, and the option should be exercised promptly.
These conditions are not in the source text but are standard Hanafi rulings.
5
Apply to a modern scenario
Test your understanding by applying the ruling to a common example, such as buying a used car with a hidden engine problem.
This step connects classical fiqh to everyday life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اذا اطلع المشترى على عيب في المبيع فهو بالخيار ان شاء اخذه بجميع الثمن وان شاء رده وليس له ان يمسكه ويأخذ النقصان.
عن عائشة ان رجلا ابتاع غلاما فاقام عنده ماشاء الله ان يقيم ثم وجد به عيبا فخاصمه الى النبي عله فرده عليه فقال الرجل يا رسول الله قد استغل غلامي فقال رسول الله الله الله الخراج بالضمان.
If the buyer discovers a defect in the purchased item, he has the choice: if he wishes, he may keep it with the full price; if he wishes, he may return it. He is not allowed to keep it and take compensation. (Hadith:) From Aisha that a man bought a slave and kept him for as long as Allah willed, then found a defect in him. He brought his case to the Prophet (peace be upon him) and he ordered him to be returned. The man said, 'O Messenger of Allah, my slave has earned profit.' The Messenger of Allah said: 'Profit is with liability.'
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage establishes the ruling on khiyar al-ayb (option for defect). When a buyer discovers a defect in the purchased item that was not known at the time of sale, he has the option to either keep the item with the full price or return it. He cannot keep the item and claim a reduction in price for the defect. The proof is the hadith of Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) where the Prophet (peace be upon him) returned a defective slave. When the seller complained that the slave had earned profit, the Prophet ruled that profit belongs to the one who bears liability ('al-kharaaj bi al-daman'), meaning the buyer keeps the profit because he bore the risk during his ownership. This hadith also indicates that the buyer must return the entire item, not just claim damages. The commentary clarifies that the defect must be one considered significant by merchants, and the buyer must not have seen the defect before purchase or agreed to it.

Key Terms
خیار العیب (khiyar al-ayb): The option for the buyer to return a purchased item if a defect is discovered after the sale.
الخراج بالضمان (al-kharaaj bi al-daman): A legal maxim meaning profits are with liability; whoever bears the risk of loss is entitled to the profits.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the buyer allowed to do if he finds a defect in the purchased item?
A) Keep the item and claim compensation for the defect
B) Return the item for a full refund or keep it with the full price
C) Return only the defective part and keep the rest
D) Demand a refund without returning the item

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the buyer of a defective item may either return it for a full refund or keep it with the full price, but cannot demand compensation while retaining the item.

Option of Defect (Khiyar al-Ayb) and Its Proof
Ruling and hadith proof for khiyar al-ayb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-ayb, identify the hadith proof, and apply the conditions for returning a defective item.

Core Idea
A buyer may return a defective item or keep it at full price, but cannot claim partial compensation.

In this lesson, we examine the option of defect (khiyar al-ayb), a buyer's right upon discovering a hidden defect. The Hanafi school relies on a hadith from Aisha (RA) where the Prophet (SAW) returned a defective slave and stated the maxim 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' (profit belongs with liability). We will explore the ruling, its proof, and the conditions that must be met.

Guided Path
1
State the Ruling
Identify the buyer's options: keep the item with full price or return it. Note the prohibition on keeping the item and claiming partial compensation.
2
Analyze the Hadith Proof
Examine the hadith from Aisha (RA): a man bought a slave, found a defect, and the Prophet (SAW) returned the slave. Extract the ruling and the maxim 'al-kharaj bi al-daman'.
The hadith is from Sunan Abi Dawud, Ibn Majah, and Bayhaqi.
3
Identify Conditions
List the conditions for khiyar al-ayb: buyer must not have seen or consented to the defect at purchase, and the defect must be considered a defect by merchants.
4
Understand the Prohibition
Recognize that the buyer cannot keep the item and claim compensation for the defect; only return or keep with full price are allowed.

فیمن اشتری عبدا فاستعمله ثم وجد به عیباج ثانی ص ۱۳۹ نمبر ۳۵۱۰) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ عیب کے ماتحت مبیع واپس کر سکتا ہے۔ اور یہ بھی معلوم ہوا کہ پوری صبح واپس کرے۔ میچ میں کوئی اضافہ نہ ہوا ہو تو نقصان وصول نہ کرے۔ اس اثر سے یہ بھی معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ پوری صبح واپس کرے گا۔ حاشیہ : (الف) حضرت عائشہ سے روایت ہے کہ ایک آدمی نے غلام خریدا۔ پس اس کے پاس جب تک اللہ نے چاہا تھہرارہا پھر اس میں عیب پایا تو حضور کے پاس جھگڑا لے گیا تو غلام کو بائع پر واپس کر دیا گیا۔ بائع نے فرمایا حضور اس نے میرے غلام کو قرض میں مبتلا کر دیا تو آپ نے فرمایا ضمان کی وجہ سے خراج لازم ہوتا ہے (ب) حضرت عائشہ سے روایت ہے کہ ایک آدمی نے غلام خریدا۔ پس اس کے پاس جب تک اللہ نے چاہا تھہرا رہا پھر اس میں عیب پایا تو حضور کے پاس جھگڑالے گیا تو غلام کو بائع پر واپس کر دیا گیا۔
Al Sharh al-Thamiri, Part 2, 34. Book of Sales. Chapter of Option of Defect. [1] (1) When the buyer discovers a defect in the sold item, he has the choice: if he wishes, he may take it with the entire price, and if he wishes, he may return it. And he may not keep it and take compensation. Chapter of Option of Defect. Necessary Note: A defect that occurs in a sale, under which the item may be returned, is called khiyar al-ayb. Its proof is in this hadith: 'From Aisha that a man bought a slave, and he kept him as long as Allah willed, then he found a defect in him, and he brought the dispute to the Prophet (peace be upon him), and he returned it to him. The man said, O Messenger of Allah, my slave has earned profits. The Messenger of Allah said: The profit belongs with the liability.' (Abu Dawud, Ibn Majah, Bayhaqi). This hadith proves that when a defect was found in the slave, he was returned to the seller. [871] (1) If the buyer becomes aware of a defect in the sold item, he has the choice: if he wishes, he takes it with the full price, and if he wishes, he returns the item. But it is not permissible for the buyer to keep the item and take compensation. The buyer took possession of the item thinking it had no defect; later the defect was discovered, so under the option of defect, he has the choice to return the entire item. But it is not that he keeps the item and takes compensation for the defect from the seller. He can return only if at the time of purchase he did not see the defect and did not consent to it. A second condition is that the defect must be one that merchants call a defect; then under the defect he can return the item. (1) The reason for returning the item is that the buyer's right was lost, so he returns the item and collects his full right. (2) In the hadith, the Companion returned the slave under defect, which indicates that under defect the item can be returned. From Aisha that a man bought a slave, and he kept him as long as Allah willed, then he found a defect in him, and he brought the dispute to the Prophet (peace be upon him), and he returned it to him. (Abu Dawud). From this hadith, it is known that under defect the item can be returned. And it is also known that the entire item should be returned. If no increase occurred in the item, he does not receive compensation. This effect also indicates that he returns the entire item. Footnote: (a) Narrated from Aisha that a man bought a slave, and he kept him as long as Allah willed, then he found a defect in him, and he brought the dispute to the Prophet, so the slave was returned to the seller. The seller said, O Messenger, he has incurred debt on my slave. He said: With liability, profit is attached. (b) Narrated from Aisha that a man bought a slave, and he kept him as long as Allah willed, then he found a defect in him, and he brought the dispute to the Prophet, so the slave was returned to the seller.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on khiyar al-ayb (option of defect). It begins by stating the rule: when a buyer discovers a defect in the purchased item, he has the choice to either accept the item with the full price or return it. He cannot keep it and claim compensation for the defect. The commentary then provides the hadith proof from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) where the Prophet (peace be upon him) returned a slave due to a defect, establishing that the item is returned entirely. The hadith 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' indicates that profits belong with the liability, so the seller is not compensated for the use of the item before discovery. The commentary clarifies that the buyer's option is conditional: (1) he must not have seen or consented to the defect at purchase, and (2) the defect must be one that merchants consider a defect. The reasoning is that the buyer's right is lost due to the defect, so he returns the full item and recovers his full price. The passage also includes a footnote repeating the hadith and emphasizing that the whole item is returned.

Key Terms
خیار العیب (khiyar al-ayb): The option given to a buyer to return a purchased item due to a defect discovered after purchase.
خراج بالضمان (al-kharaj bi al-daman): A legal maxim meaning that the profits derived from an item belong to the one who bears liability for it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the buyer allowed to do upon discovering a hidden defect in a purchased item?
A) He may keep the item and claim a partial refund.
B) He may return the item or keep it with full price, but cannot claim compensation.
C) He must return the item immediately.
D) He may demand a replacement without returning the item.

Key Takeaway
A buyer discovering a hidden defect may either return the item for a full refund or keep it at full price, but cannot claim partial compensation.

Definition of Defect ('Ayb) According to Custom
Definition of 'ayb by merchant custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of a defect in sales and apply it to a new scenario using merchant custom as the criterion.

Core Idea
A defect is whatever merchants customarily consider a price‑reducing flaw.

When a product has a hidden flaw, the buyer may return it under the option of defect (khiyar al-ayb). But what exactly counts as a defect? The Hanafi school does not rely on a fixed list; instead, it looks to the custom of merchants. This lesson unpacks that definition and teaches you how to identify a defect in practice.

Guided Path
1
Locate the key phrase
Read the Arabic sentence: 'wa kull ma awjaba nuqsan al-thaman fi adat al-tujjar fahuwa 'ayb'. Identify the three components: (1) 'kull ma awjaba' (everything that necessitates), (2) 'nuqsan al-thaman' (decrease in price), (3) 'fi adat al-tujjar' (in the custom of merchants).
The definition is composite: it links an objective effect (price reduction) to a subjective source (custom).
2
Understand the role of custom
The phrase 'fi adat al-tujjar' is the pivot. The Hanafi school holds that what constitutes a defect is not fixed by the sacred texts but is determined by the practice of merchants in a given time and place. This makes the definition dynamic.
Contrast with schools that list specific defects (e.g., Shafi'i lists: leprosy, blindness, etc. in slaves).
3
Apply the definition
Given a new scenario, ask: Would the presence of this attribute reduce the market price of the item according to the accepted custom of merchants in that locality? If yes, it is a defect; if no, it is not.
The buyer must prove the price reduction, often by expert testimony or market evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۸۷۲](۲) وكل ما اوجب نقصان الثمن فى عادة التجار فهو عيب. هر وہ عیب جو شمن کا نقصان واجب کرتا ہو تاجروں کی عادت میں وہ عیب ہے۔ تا جر جس کو عیب کہتے ہوں اور جس عیب کی وجہ سے قیمت میں کمی واقع ہو جاتی ہو وہ عیب ہے۔ اصول عیب میں وہاں کے محاورے کا اعتبار ہے۔
[872] (2) And everything that obligates a decrease in the price in the custom of merchants is a defect. In Urdu: Every defect that necessitates a decrease in the price in the custom of merchants is a defect. That which merchants call a defect and because of which a reduction in price occurs is a defect. The principle regarding defect is that the local idiom is to be considered.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage defines what constitutes a 'ayb (defect) in the context of khiyar al-ayb. The ruling states that any attribute that reduces the price according to the custom of merchants is considered a defect. The Urdu commentary elaborates that the definition relies on the practice of traders: whatever they consider a defect and causes a price reduction is a defect. The principle (usul) is that the common usage of the locality is taken into account. Thus, the definition is not fixed by the text but varies with time and place based on merchant custom.

Key Terms
عیب (ayb): A flaw or attribute that reduces the market price of an item in the custom of merchants.
عادة التجار (adat al-tujjar): The custom and practice of merchants in a given locality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the principle (usul) regarding the definition of a defect?
A) It is determined by a fixed list in the Hadith.
B) It is based on the local idiom (mahawara) of the people.
C) It depends on the buyer's personal preference.
D) It is defined solely by the seller's disclosure.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a defect is whatever the local custom of merchants considers to be a price‑reducing flaw.
